<div style="margin-top: 0.3em; text-align: center;"><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Chemical_infobox#References" title="Wikipedia:Chemical infobox">Infobox references</a></div>
</td></tr>
</tbody></table><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Chemical compound</div>
<p><b>Carbon dioxide</b> (<a href="/wiki/Chemical_formula" title="Chemical formula">chemical formula</a> <b>CO<sub style="font-size: 80%;vertical-align: -0.35em">2</sub></b>) is a <a href="/wiki/Chemical_compound" title="Chemical compound">chemical compound</a> made up of <a href="/wiki/Molecule" title="Molecule">molecules</a> that each have one <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a> atom <a href="/wiki/Covalent_bond" title="Covalent bond">covalently</a> <a href="/wiki/Double_bond" title="Double bond">double bonded</a> to two <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> atoms. It is found in the gas state at room temperature.
</p><p>In the air, carbon dioxide is transparent to visible light but absorbs <a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">infrared radiation</a>, acting as a <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gas</a>. It is a <a href="/wiki/Trace_gas" title="Trace gas">trace gas</a> <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_in_Earth%27s_atmosphere" title="Carbon dioxide in Earth's atmosphere">in Earth's atmosphere</a> at 417 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parts_per_million" title="Parts per million">ppm</a> (about 0.04%) by volume, having risen from pre-industrial levels of 280 ppm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cambridge2013_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cambridge2013-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> Burning <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">fossil fuels</a> is the primary cause of these increased CO<sub>2</sub> concentrations and also the primary cause of <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">global warming and climate change</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> Carbon dioxide is soluble in water and is found in <a href="/wiki/Groundwater" title="Groundwater">groundwater</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lake" title="Lake">lakes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ice_cap" title="Ice cap">ice caps</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Seawater" title="Seawater">seawater</a>. When carbon dioxide dissolves in water it forms <a href="/wiki/Carbonic_acid" title="Carbonic acid">carbonic acid</a> (H<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>), which causes <a href="/wiki/Ocean_acidification" title="Ocean acidification">ocean acidification</a> as atmospheric CO<sub>2</sub> levels increase.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NRC2010_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-NRC2010-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p><p>As the source of available carbon in the <a href="/wiki/Carbon_cycle" title="Carbon cycle">carbon cycle</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atmospheric_carbon_dioxide" title="Atmospheric carbon dioxide">atmospheric carbon dioxide</a> is the primary carbon source for <a href="/wiki/Life" title="Life">life</a> on Earth. Its concentration in Earth's pre-industrial atmosphere since late in the <a href="/wiki/Precambrian" title="Precambrian">Precambrian</a> has been regulated by organisms and geological phenomena. <a href="/wiki/Plant" title="Plant">Plants</a>, <a href="/wiki/Algae" title="Algae">algae</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cyanobacteria" title="Cyanobacteria">cyanobacteria</a> use <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">energy</a> from <a href="/wiki/Sunlight" title="Sunlight">sunlight</a> to synthesize <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">carbohydrates</a> from carbon dioxide and water in a process called <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthesis</a>, which produces oxygen as a waste product.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> In turn, oxygen is consumed and CO<sub>2</sub> is released as waste by all <a href="/wiki/Aerobic_organism" title="Aerobic organism">aerobic organisms</a> when they metabolize <a href="/wiki/Organic_compound" title="Organic compound">organic compounds</a> to produce energy by <a href="/wiki/Cellular_respiration" title="Cellular respiration">respiration</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> CO<sub>2</sub> is released from organic materials when they <a href="/wiki/Decomposition" title="Decomposition">decay</a> or combust, such as in forest fires. Since plants require CO<sub>2</sub> for photosynthesis, and humans and animals depend on plants for food, CO<sub>2</sub> is necessary for the survival of life on earth.
</p><p>Carbon dioxide is 53% more dense than dry air, but is long lived and thoroughly mixes in the atmosphere. About half of excess CO<sub>2</sub> emissions to the atmosphere are absorbed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbon_fixation" title="Carbon fixation">land</a> and ocean <a href="/wiki/Carbon_sink" title="Carbon sink">carbon sinks</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> These sinks can become saturated and are volatile, as decay and <a href="/wiki/Wildfire" title="Wildfire">wildfires</a> result in the CO<sub>2</sub> being released back into the atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> CO<sub>2</sub> is eventually <a href="/wiki/Carbon_sequestration" title="Carbon sequestration">sequestered</a> (stored for the long term) in rocks and organic deposits like <a href="/wiki/Coal" title="Coal">coal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">petroleum</a> and <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a>. Sequestered CO<sub>2</sub> is released into the atmosphere through burning fossil fuels or naturally by <a href="/wiki/Volcano" title="Volcano">volcanoes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hot_spring" title="Hot spring">hot springs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Geyser" title="Geyser">geysers</a>, and when <a href="/wiki/Carbonate_rock" title="Carbonate rock">carbonate rocks</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dissolution_(chemistry)" title="Dissolution (chemistry)">dissolve</a> in water or react with acids.
</p><p>CO<sub>2</sub> is a versatile industrial material, used, for example, as an inert gas in welding and <a href="/wiki/Fire_extinguisher" title="Fire extinguisher">fire extinguishers</a>, as a pressurizing gas in air guns and oil recovery, and as a supercritical fluid solvent in decaffeination of coffee and <a href="/wiki/Supercritical_drying" title="Supercritical drying">supercritical drying</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tsotsas_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tsotsas-17">[17]</a></sup> It is also a <a href="/wiki/Raw_material" title="Raw material">feedstock</a> for the synthesis of fuels and chemicals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> It is an unwanted byproduct in many large scale <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxidation" title="Oxidation">oxidation</a> processes, for example, in the production of <a href="/wiki/Acrylic_acid" title="Acrylic acid">acrylic acid</a> (over 5 million tons/year).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> The frozen solid form of CO<sub>2</sub>, known as <a href="/wiki/Dry_ice" title="Dry ice">dry ice</a>, is used as a refrigerant and as an abrasive in <a href="/wiki/Dry-ice_blasting" title="Dry-ice blasting">dry-ice blasting</a>. It is a byproduct of <a href="/wiki/Fermentation" title="Fermentation">fermentation</a> of sugars in <a href="/wiki/Bread" title="Bread">bread</a>, <a href="/wiki/Beer" title="Beer">beer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wine" title="Wine">wine</a> making, and is added to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbonated_beverage" title="Carbonated beverage">carbonated beverages</a> like <a href="/wiki/Carbonated_water" title="Carbonated water">seltzer</a> and beer for effervescence. It has a sharp and acidic odor and generates the taste of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soda_water" title="Soda water">soda water</a> in the mouth,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> but at normally encountered concentrations it is odorless.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AirProductsMSDS_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-AirProductsMSDS-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Carbon-dioxide-crystal-3D-vdW.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1066" data-file-width="1100" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Carbon-dioxide-crystal-3D-vdW.png/170px-Carbon-dioxide-crystal-3D-vdW.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Carbon-dioxide-crystal-3D-vdW.png/255px-Carbon-dioxide-crystal-3D-vdW.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Carbon-dioxide-crystal-3D-vdW.png/340px-Carbon-dioxide-crystal-3D-vdW.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Carbon-dioxide-crystal-3D-vdW.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Crystal structure of <a href="/wiki/Dry_ice" title="Dry ice">dry ice</a></div></div></div>
<p>Carbon dioxide was the first gas to be described as a discrete substance. In about 1640,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Flemish_people" title="Flemish people">Flemish</a> chemist <a href="/wiki/Jan_Baptist_van_Helmont" title="Jan Baptist van Helmont">Jan Baptist van Helmont</a> observed that when he burned <a href="/wiki/Charcoal" title="Charcoal">charcoal</a> in a closed vessel, the mass of the resulting <a href="/wiki/Ash_(analytical_chemistry)" title="Ash (analytical chemistry)">ash</a> was much less than that of the original charcoal. His interpretation was that the rest of the charcoal had been transmuted into an invisible substance he termed a "gas" or "wild spirit" (<i>spiritus sylvestris</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p><p>The properties of carbon dioxide were further studied in the 1750s by the <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scottish</a> physician <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Black" title="Joseph Black">Joseph Black</a>. He found that <a href="/wiki/Limestone" title="Limestone">limestone</a> (<a href="/wiki/Calcium_carbonate" title="Calcium carbonate">calcium carbonate</a>) could be heated or treated with <a href="/wiki/Acid" title="Acid">acids</a> to yield a gas he called "fixed air." He observed that the fixed air was denser than air and supported neither flame nor animal life. Black also found that when bubbled through <a href="/wiki/Limewater" title="Limewater">limewater</a> (a saturated aqueous solution of <a href="/wiki/Calcium_hydroxide" title="Calcium hydroxide">calcium hydroxide</a>), it would <a href="/wiki/Precipitation_(chemistry)" title="Precipitation (chemistry)">precipitate</a> calcium carbonate. He used this phenomenon to illustrate that carbon dioxide is produced by animal respiration and microbial fermentation. In 1772, English chemist <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Priestley" title="Joseph Priestley">Joseph Priestley</a> published a paper entitled <i>Impregnating Water with Fixed Air</i> in which he described a process of dripping <a href="/wiki/Sulfuric_acid" title="Sulfuric acid">sulfuric acid</a> (or <i>oil of vitriol</i> as Priestley knew it) on chalk in order to produce carbon dioxide, and forcing the gas to dissolve by agitating a bowl of water in contact with the gas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Priestley_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Priestley-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p><p>Carbon dioxide was first liquefied (at elevated pressures) in 1823 by <a href="/wiki/Humphry_Davy" title="Humphry Davy">Humphry Davy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Michael_Faraday" title="Michael Faraday">Michael Faraday</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davy_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Davy-29">[29]</a></sup> The earliest description of solid carbon dioxide (<a href="/wiki/Dry_ice" title="Dry ice">dry ice</a>) was given by the French inventor <a href="/wiki/Adrien-Jean-Pierre_Thilorier" title="Adrien-Jean-Pierre Thilorier">Adrien-Jean-Pierre Thilorier</a>, who in 1835 opened a pressurized container of liquid carbon dioxide, only to find that the cooling produced by the rapid evaporation of the liquid yielded a "snow" of solid CO<sub>2</sub>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemical_and_physical_properties">Chemical and physical properties</span></h2>
<h3><span id="Structure.2C_bonding_and_molecular_vibrations"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Structure,_bonding_and_molecular_vibrations">Structure, bonding and molecular vibrations</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Molecular_orbital_diagram#Carbon_dioxide" title="Molecular orbital diagram">Molecular orbital diagram § Carbon dioxide</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Molecular_symmetry" title="Molecular symmetry">symmetry</a> of a carbon dioxide molecule is linear and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Centrosymmetric" title="Centrosymmetric">centrosymmetric</a> at its equilibrium geometry. The <a href="/wiki/Bond_length" title="Bond length">length</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbon-oxygen_bond" title="Carbon-oxygen bond">carbon-oxygen bond</a> in carbon dioxide is 116.3 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Picometer" title="Picometer">pm</a>, noticeably shorter than the roughly 140-pm length of a typical single C–O bond, and shorter than most other C–O multiply-bonded <a href="/wiki/Functional_group" title="Functional group">functional groups</a> such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbonyls" title="Carbonyls">carbonyls</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Green_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Green-32">[32]</a></sup> Since it is centrosymmetric, the molecule has no <a href="/wiki/Electric_dipole_moment" title="Electric dipole moment">electric dipole moment</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Co2_vibrations.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="319" data-file-width="886" decoding="async" height="79" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Co2_vibrations.svg/220px-Co2_vibrations.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Co2_vibrations.svg/330px-Co2_vibrations.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Co2_vibrations.svg/440px-Co2_vibrations.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Co2_vibrations.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Infrared_spectroscopy#Number_of_vibrational_modes" title="Infrared spectroscopy">Stretching and bending oscillations</a> of the <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1060974363">.mw-parser-output .template-chem2-su{display:inline-block;font-size:80%;line-height:1;vertical-align:-0.35em}.mw-parser-output .template-chem2-su>span{display:block}.mw-parser-output sub.template-chem2-sub{font-size:80%;vertical-align:-0.35em}.mw-parser-output sup.template-chem2-sup{font-size:80%;vertical-align:0.65em}</style><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub></span> carbon dioxide molecule. Upper left: symmetric stretching. Upper right: antisymmetric stretching. Lower line: degenerate pair of bending modes.</div></div></div>
<p>As a linear triatomic molecule, <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub></span> has four <a href="/wiki/Molecular_vibration" title="Molecular vibration">vibrational modes</a> as shown in the diagram. In the symmetric and the antisymmetric stretching modes, the atoms move along the axis of the molecule. There are two bending modes, which are <a href="/wiki/Degenerate_energy_levels" title="Degenerate energy levels">degenerate</a>, meaning that they have the same frequency and same energy, because of the symmetry of the molecule. When a molecule touches a surface or touches another molecule, the two bending modes can differ in frequency because the interaction is different for the two modes. Some of the vibrational modes are observed in the <a href="/wiki/Infrared_spectroscopy" title="Infrared spectroscopy">infrared (IR) spectrum</a>: the antisymmetric stretching mode at <a href="/wiki/Wavenumber" title="Wavenumber">wavenumber</a> 2349 cm<sup>−1</sup> (wavelength 4.25 μm) and the degenerate pair of bending modes at 667 cm<sup>−1</sup> (wavelength 15 μm). The symmetric stretching mode does not create an electric dipole so is not observed in IR spectroscopy, but it is detected in by <a href="/wiki/Raman_spectroscopy" title="Raman spectroscopy">Raman spectroscopy</a> at 1388 cm<sup>−1</sup> (wavelength 7.2 μm).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the gas phase, carbon dioxide molecules undergo significant vibrational motions and do not keep a fixed structure. However, in a <a href="/wiki/Coulomb_explosion#Coulomb_Explosion_Imaging" title="Coulomb explosion">Coulomb explosion imaging</a> experiment, an instantaneous image of the molecular structure can be deduced. Such an experiment<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> has been performed for carbon dioxide.
The result of this experiment, and the conclusion of theoretical calculations<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> based on an <a href="/wiki/Ab_initio_quantum_chemistry_methods" title="Ab initio quantum chemistry methods">ab initio</a> <a href="/wiki/Potential_energy_surface" title="Potential energy surface">potential energy surface</a> of the molecule, is that none of the molecules in the gas phase are ever exactly linear.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_aqueous_solution">In aqueous solution</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Carbonic_acid" title="Carbonic acid">Carbonic acid</a></div>
<p>Carbon dioxide is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soluble" title="Soluble">soluble</a> in water, in which it reversibly forms <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">H<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub>CO<sub class="template-chem2-sub">3</sub></span> (carbonic acid), which is a <a href="/wiki/Acid_strength" title="Acid strength">weak acid</a> since its ionization in water is incomplete.
</p>

<p>Hence, the majority of the carbon dioxide is not converted into carbonic acid, but remains as <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub></span> molecules, not affecting the pH.
</p><p>The relative concentrations of <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub></span>, <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">H<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub>CO<sub class="template-chem2-sub">3</sub></span>, and the <a href="/wiki/Deprotonation" title="Deprotonation">deprotonated</a> forms <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">HCO<span class="template-chem2-su"><span>−</span><span>3</span></span></span> (<a href="/wiki/Bicarbonate" title="Bicarbonate">bicarbonate</a>) and <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span class="template-chem2-su"><span>2−</span><span>3</span></span></span>(<a href="/wiki/Carbonate" title="Carbonate">carbonate</a>) depend on the <a href="/wiki/PH" title="PH">pH</a>. As shown in a <a href="/wiki/Bjerrum_plot" title="Bjerrum plot">Bjerrum plot</a>, in neutral or slightly alkaline water (pH &gt; 6.5), the bicarbonate form predominates (&gt;50%) becoming the most prevalent (&gt;95%) at the pH of seawater. In very alkaline water (pH &gt; 10.4), the predominant (&gt;50%) form is carbonate. The oceans, being mildly alkaline with typical pH = 8.2–8.5, contain about 120 mg of bicarbonate per liter.
</p><p>Being <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diprotic_acid" title="Diprotic acid">diprotic</a>, carbonic acid has two <a href="/wiki/Acid_dissociation_constant" title="Acid dissociation constant">acid dissociation constants</a>, the first one for the dissociation into the bicarbonate (also called hydrogen carbonate) ion (<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">HCO<span class="template-chem2-su"><span>−</span><span>3</span></span></span>):
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\ce {H2CO3 &lt;=&gt; HCO3- + H+}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<p>Since most of the dissolved <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub></span>remains as <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub></span> molecules, <i>K</i><sub>a1</sub>(apparent) has a much larger denominator and a much smaller value than the true <i>K</i><sub>a1</sub>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p><p>The bicarbonate ion is an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amphoteric" title="Amphoteric">amphoteric</a> species that can act as an acid or as a base, depending on pH of the solution. At high pH, it dissociates significantly into the <a href="/wiki/Carbonate" title="Carbonate">carbonate</a> ion (<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span class="template-chem2-su"><span>2−</span><span>3</span></span></span>):
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\ce {HCO3- &lt;=&gt; CO3^2- + H+}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<p>In organisms carbonic acid production is catalysed by the <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzyme</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carbonic_anhydrase" title="Carbonic anhydrase">carbonic anhydrase</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemical_reactions_of_CO2">Chemical reactions of CO<sub>2</sub></span></h3>
<p><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub></span> is a potent <a href="/wiki/Electrophile" title="Electrophile">electrophile</a> having an electrophilic reactivity that is comparable to <a href="/wiki/Benzaldehyde" title="Benzaldehyde">benzaldehyde</a> or strong <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%CE%91,%CE%B2-unsaturated_carbonyl_compound" title="Α,β-unsaturated carbonyl compound">α,β-unsaturated carbonyl compounds</a>. However, unlike electrophiles of similar reactivity, the reactions of nucleophiles with <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub></span> are thermodynamically less favored and are often found to be highly reversible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> Only very strong nucleophiles, like the  <a href="/wiki/Carbanion" title="Carbanion">carbanions</a> provided by  <a href="/wiki/Grignard_reagent" title="Grignard reagent">Grignard reagents</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organolithium_compound" title="Organolithium compound">organolithium compounds</a> react with <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub></span> to give <a href="/wiki/Carboxylate" title="Carboxylate">carboxylates</a>:
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\ce {MR + CO2 -&gt; RCO2M}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<dd>where M = <a href="/wiki/Lithium" title="Lithium">Li</a> or <a href="/wiki/Magnesium" title="Magnesium">Mg</a> <a href="/wiki/Bromine" title="Bromine">Br</a> and R = <a href="/wiki/Alkyl" title="Alkyl">alkyl</a> or <a href="/wiki/Aryl" title="Aryl">aryl</a>.</dd></dl>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Metal_carbon_dioxide_complex" title="Metal carbon dioxide complex">metal carbon dioxide complexes</a>, <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub></span> serves as a <a href="/wiki/Ligand" title="Ligand">ligand</a>, which can facilitate the conversion of <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub></span> to other chemicals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup>
</p><p>The reduction of <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub></span> to <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">CO</a> is ordinarily a difficult and slow reaction:
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\ce {CO2 + 2 e- + 2H+ -&gt; CO + H2O}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photoautotrophs" title="Photoautotrophs">Photoautotrophs</a> (i.e. <a href="/wiki/Plant" title="Plant">plants</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cyanobacteria" title="Cyanobacteria">cyanobacteria</a>) use the energy contained in sunlight to <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthesize</a> simple <a href="/wiki/Sugar" title="Sugar">sugars</a> from <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub></span> absorbed from the air and water:

The nickel-containing enzyme <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide_dehydrogenase" title="Carbon monoxide dehydrogenase">carbon monoxide dehydrogenase</a> catalyses this process.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_properties">Physical properties</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_data" title="Carbon dioxide data">Carbon dioxide data</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dry_Ice_Pellets_Subliming.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="523" data-file-width="609" decoding="async" height="189" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Dry_Ice_Pellets_Subliming.jpg/220px-Dry_Ice_Pellets_Subliming.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Dry_Ice_Pellets_Subliming.jpg/330px-Dry_Ice_Pellets_Subliming.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Dry_Ice_Pellets_Subliming.jpg/440px-Dry_Ice_Pellets_Subliming.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dry_Ice_Pellets_Subliming.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Pellets of "dry ice", a common form of solid carbon dioxide</div></div></div>
<p>Carbon dioxide is colorless. At low concentrations the gas is odorless; however, at sufficiently high concentrations, it has a sharp, acidic odor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AirProductsMSDS_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-AirProductsMSDS-1">[1]</a></sup> At <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Standard_conditions_for_temperature_and_pressure" title="Standard conditions for temperature and pressure">standard temperature and pressure</a>, the density of carbon dioxide is around 1.98 kg/m<sup>3</sup>, about 1.53 times that of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth%27s_atmosphere" title="Earth's atmosphere">air</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p><p>Carbon dioxide has no liquid state at pressures below <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7005517950000000000♠"></span>0.517<span style="margin-left:.25em;">95</span>(10) MPa</span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Span_1999_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-Span_1999-2">[2]</a></sup> (<span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7005517950095250000♠"></span>5.111<span style="margin-left:.25em;">77</span>(99) atm</span>). At a pressure of 1 <a href="/wiki/Standard_atmosphere_(unit)" title="Standard atmosphere (unit)">atm</a> (<span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7005101325000000000♠"></span>0.101<span style="margin-left:.25em;">325</span> MPa</span>), the gas <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deposition_(physics)" title="Deposition (physics)">deposits</a> directly to a solid at temperatures below <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7002194685500000000♠"></span>194.6855(30) K</span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Span_1999_2-3"><a href="#cite_note-Span_1999-2">[2]</a></sup> (<span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7002194685500000000♠"></span>−78.4645(30) °C</span>) and the solid <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sublimation_(chemistry)" title="Sublimation (chemistry)">sublimes</a> directly to a gas above this temperature. In its solid state, carbon dioxide is commonly called <a href="/wiki/Dry_ice" title="Dry ice">dry ice</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Carbon_dioxide_pressure-temperature_phase_diagram.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="700" data-file-width="742" decoding="async" height="236" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Carbon_dioxide_pressure-temperature_phase_diagram.svg/250px-Carbon_dioxide_pressure-temperature_phase_diagram.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Carbon_dioxide_pressure-temperature_phase_diagram.svg/375px-Carbon_dioxide_pressure-temperature_phase_diagram.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Carbon_dioxide_pressure-temperature_phase_diagram.svg/500px-Carbon_dioxide_pressure-temperature_phase_diagram.svg.png 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Carbon_dioxide_pressure-temperature_phase_diagram.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Pressure–temperature <a href="/wiki/Phase_diagram" title="Phase diagram">phase diagram</a> of carbon dioxide. Note that it is a log-lin chart.</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Liquid_carbon_dioxide" title="Liquid carbon dioxide">Liquid carbon dioxide</a> forms only at <a href="/wiki/Pressure" title="Pressure">pressures</a> above <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7005517950000000000♠"></span>0.517<span style="margin-left:.25em;">95</span>(10) MPa</span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Span_1999_2-4"><a href="#cite_note-Span_1999-2">[2]</a></sup> (<span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7005517950095250000♠"></span>5.111<span style="margin-left:.25em;">77</span>(99) atm</span>); the <a href="/wiki/Triple_point" title="Triple point">triple point</a> of carbon dioxide is <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7002216592000000000♠"></span>216.592(3) K</span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Span_1999_2-5"><a href="#cite_note-Span_1999-2">[2]</a></sup> (<span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7002216592000000000♠"></span>−56.558(3) °C</span>) at <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7005517950000000000♠"></span>0.517<span style="margin-left:.25em;">95</span>(10) MPa</span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Span_1999_2-6"><a href="#cite_note-Span_1999-2">[2]</a></sup> (<span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7005517950095250000♠"></span>5.111<span style="margin-left:.25em;">77</span>(99) atm</span>) (see phase diagram). The <a href="/wiki/Critical_point_(thermodynamics)" title="Critical point (thermodynamics)">critical point</a> is <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7002304128000000000♠"></span>304.128(15) K</span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Span_1999_2-7"><a href="#cite_note-Span_1999-2">[2]</a></sup> (<span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7002304128000000000♠"></span>30.978(15) °C</span>) at <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7006737730000000000♠"></span>7.3773(30) MPa</span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Span_1999_2-8"><a href="#cite_note-Span_1999-2">[2]</a></sup> (<span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7006737727060000000♠"></span>72.808(30) atm</span>). Another form of solid carbon dioxide observed at high pressure is an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amorphous" title="Amorphous">amorphous</a> glass-like solid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> This form of glass, called <i><a href="/wiki/Amorphous_carbonia" title="Amorphous carbonia">carbonia</a></i>, is produced by <a href="/wiki/Supercooling" title="Supercooling">supercooling</a> heated <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub></span> at extreme pressures (40–48 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GPa" title="GPa">GPa</a>, or about 400,000 atmospheres) in a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diamond_anvil" title="Diamond anvil">diamond anvil</a>. This discovery confirmed the theory that carbon dioxide could exist in a glass state similar to other members of its elemental family, like <a href="/wiki/Silicon_dioxide" title="Silicon dioxide">silicon dioxide</a> (silica glass) and <a href="/wiki/Germanium_dioxide" title="Germanium dioxide">germanium dioxide</a>. Unlike silica and germania glasses, however, carbonia glass is not stable at normal pressures and reverts to gas when pressure is released.
</p><p>At temperatures and pressures above the critical point, carbon dioxide behaves as a <a href="/wiki/Supercritical_fluid" title="Supercritical fluid">supercritical fluid</a> known as <a href="/wiki/Supercritical_carbon_dioxide" title="Supercritical carbon dioxide">supercritical carbon dioxide</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Isolation_and_production">Isolation and production</span></h2>
<p><span class="anchor" id="CO2_production"></span>
Carbon dioxide can be obtained by <a href="/wiki/Distillation" title="Distillation">distillation</a> from air, but the method is inefficient. Industrially, carbon dioxide is predominantly an unrecovered waste product, produced by several methods which may be practiced at various scales.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kirk_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-kirk-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Combustion" title="Combustion">combustion</a> of all <a href="/wiki/Carbon-based_fuel" title="Carbon-based fuel">carbon-based fuels</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a> (<a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a>), petroleum distillates (<a href="/wiki/Gasoline" title="Gasoline">gasoline</a>, <a href="/wiki/Diesel_fuel" title="Diesel fuel">diesel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kerosene" title="Kerosene">kerosene</a>, <a href="/wiki/Propane" title="Propane">propane</a>), coal, wood and generic organic matter produces carbon dioxide and, except in the case of pure carbon, water. As an example, the chemical reaction between methane and <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a>:
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\ce {CH4 + 2 O2-&gt; CO2 + 2 H2O}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<p><a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">Iron</a> is reduced from its oxides with <a href="/wiki/Coke_(fuel)" title="Coke (fuel)">coke</a> in a <a href="/wiki/Blast_furnace" title="Blast furnace">blast furnace</a>, producing <a href="/wiki/Pig_iron" title="Pig iron">pig iron</a> and carbon dioxide:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p><p>Carbon dioxide is a byproduct of the industrial production of hydrogen by <a href="/wiki/Steam_reforming" title="Steam reforming">steam reforming</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Water_gas_shift_reaction" title="Water gas shift reaction">water gas shift reaction</a> in <a href="/wiki/Ammonia_production" title="Ammonia production">ammonia production</a>. These processes begin with the reaction of water and natural gas (mainly methane).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> This is a major source of food-grade carbon dioxide for use in carbonation of <a href="/wiki/Beer" title="Beer">beer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Soft_drink" title="Soft drink">soft drinks</a>, and is also used for stunning animals such as <a href="/wiki/Poultry" title="Poultry">poultry</a>. In the summer of 2018 a shortage of carbon dioxide for these purposes arose in Europe due to the temporary shut-down of several ammonia plants for maintenance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Carbonates">Carbonates</span></h3>
<p>It is produced by thermal decomposition of limestone, <span class="chemf nowrap">CaCO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span> by heating (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Calcining" title="Calcining">calcining</a>) at about 850 °C (1,560 °F), in the manufacture of <a href="/wiki/Calcium_oxide" title="Calcium oxide">quicklime</a> (<a href="/wiki/Calcium_oxide" title="Calcium oxide">calcium oxide</a>, <span class="chemf nowrap">CaO</span>), a compound that has many industrial uses:

<p>Such reactions are accompanied by foaming or bubbling, or both, as the gas is released. They have widespread uses in industry because they can be used to neutralize waste acid streams.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fermentation">Fermentation</span></h3>
<p>Carbon dioxide is a by-product of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fermentation_(biochemistry)" title="Fermentation (biochemistry)">fermentation</a> of sugar in the <a href="/wiki/Brewing" title="Brewing">brewing</a> of <a href="/wiki/Beer" title="Beer">beer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Whisky" title="Whisky">whisky</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Alcoholic_beverage" title="Alcoholic beverage">alcoholic beverages</a> and in the production of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bioethanol" title="Bioethanol">bioethanol</a>. <a href="/wiki/Yeast" title="Yeast">Yeast</a> metabolizes sugar to produce <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub></span> and <a href="/wiki/Ethanol" title="Ethanol">ethanol</a>, also known as alcohol, as follows:

<p>All <a href="/wiki/Cellular_respiration" title="Cellular respiration">aerobic</a> organisms produce <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1060974363" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<sub class="template-chem2-sub">2</sub></span> when they oxidize <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">carbohydrates</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fatty_acid" title="Fatty acid">fatty acids</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">proteins</a>. The large number of reactions involved are exceedingly complex and not described easily. Refer to (<a href="/wiki/Cellular_respiration" title="Cellular respiration">cellular respiration</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anaerobic_respiration" title="Anaerobic respiration">anaerobic respiration</a> and <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthesis</a>). The equation for the respiration of glucose and other <a href="/wiki/Monosaccharide" title="Monosaccharide">monosaccharides</a> is:
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\ce {C6H12O6 + 6 O2 -&gt; 6 CO2 + 6 H2O}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">

<mtext>O</mtext>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\ce {C6H12O6 + 6 O2 -&gt; 6 CO2 + 6 H2O}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle {\ce {C6H12O6 + 6 O2 -&gt; 6 CO2 + 6 H2O}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/0ab8319934fbda5d103b6aece6bccebdb59a01ae" style="vertical-align: -1.005ex; width:36.648ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anaerobic_organisms" title="Anaerobic organisms">Anaerobic organisms</a> decompose organic material producing methane and carbon dioxide together with traces of other compounds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> Regardless of the type of organic material, the production of gases follows well defined <a href="/wiki/Chemical_kinetics" title="Chemical kinetics">kinetic pattern</a>. Carbon dioxide comprises about 40–45% of the gas that emanates from decomposition in landfills (termed "<a href="/wiki/Landfill_gas" title="Landfill gas">landfill gas</a>"). Most of the remaining 50–55% is methane.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Applications">Applications</span></h2>
<p>Carbon dioxide is used by the food industry, the oil industry, and the chemical industry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kirk_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-kirk-42">[42]</a></sup>
The compound has varied commercial uses but one of its greatest uses as a chemical is in the production of carbonated beverages; it provides the sparkle in carbonated beverages such as soda water, beer and sparkling wine.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Precursor_to_chemicals">Precursor to chemicals</span></h3>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1097763485">.mw-parser-output .ambox{border:1px solid #a2a9b1;border-left:10px solid #36c;background-color:#fbfbfb;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .ambox+link+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+link+style+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+link+link+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+.mw-empty-elt+link+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+.mw-empty-elt+link+style+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+.mw-empty-elt+link+link+.ambox{margin-top:-1px}html body.mediawiki .mw-parser-output .ambox.mbox-small-left{margin:4px 1em 4px 0;overflow:hidden;width:238px;border-collapse:collapse;font-size:88%;line-height:1.25em}.mw-parser-output .ambox-speedy{border-left:10px solid #b32424;background-color:#fee7e6}.mw-parser-output .ambox-delete{border-left:10px solid #b32424}.mw-parser-output .ambox-content{border-left:10px solid #f28500}.mw-parser-output .ambox-style{border-left:10px solid #fc3}.mw-parser-output .ambox-move{border-left:10px solid #9932cc}.mw-parser-output .ambox-protection{border-left:10px solid #a2a9b1}.mw-parser-output .ambox .mbox-text{border:none;padding:0.25em .mbox-text-span{margin-left:23px!important}@media(min-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .ambox{margin:0 10%}}</style><table class="box-Expand_section plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg"><img alt="[icon]" data-file-height="31" data-file-width="44" decoding="async" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/20px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/30px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/40px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 2x" width="20"/></a></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs expansion</b>. You can help by <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Carbon_dioxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=">adding to it</a>.  <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">July 2014</span>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>In the chemical industry, carbon dioxide is mainly consumed as an ingredient in the production of <a href="/wiki/Urea" title="Urea">urea</a>, with a smaller fraction being used to produce <a href="/wiki/Methanol" title="Methanol">methanol</a> and a range of other products.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> Some carboxylic acid derivatives such as <a href="/wiki/Sodium_salicylate" title="Sodium salicylate">sodium salicylate</a> are prepared using CO<sub>2</sub> by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kolbe-Schmitt_reaction" title="Kolbe-Schmitt reaction">Kolbe-Schmitt reaction</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p><p>In addition to conventional processes using CO<sub>2</sub> for chemical production, electrochemical methods are also being explored at a research level. In particular, the use of renewable energy for production of fuels from CO<sub>2</sub> (such as methanol) is attractive as this could result in fuels that could be easily transported and used within conventional combustion technologies but have no net CO<sub>2</sub> emissions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Agriculture">Agriculture</span></h3>
<p>Plants require carbon dioxide to conduct photosynthesis. The atmospheres of greenhouses may (if of large size, must) be enriched with additional CO<sub>2</sub> to sustain and increase the rate of plant growth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> At very high concentrations (100 times atmospheric concentration, or greater), carbon dioxide can be toxic to animal life, so raising the concentration to 10,000 ppm (1%) or higher for several hours will eliminate pests such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Whiteflies" title="Whiteflies">whiteflies</a> and <a href="/wiki/Spider_mite" title="Spider mite">spider mites</a> in a greenhouse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foods">Foods</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Soda_bubbles_macro.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="750" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Soda_bubbles_macro.jpg/220px-Soda_bubbles_macro.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Soda_bubbles_macro.jpg/330px-Soda_bubbles_macro.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Soda_bubbles_macro.jpg/440px-Soda_bubbles_macro.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Soda_bubbles_macro.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Carbon dioxide bubbles in a soft drink</div></div></div>
<p>Carbon dioxide is a <a href="/wiki/Food_additive" title="Food additive">food additive</a> used as a propellant and acidity regulator in the food industry. It is approved for usage in the EU<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> (listed as <a href="/wiki/E_number" title="E number">E number</a> E290), US<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> and Australia and New Zealand<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> (listed by its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/INS_number" title="INS number">INS number</a> 290).
</p><p>A candy called <a href="/wiki/Pop_Rocks" title="Pop Rocks">Pop Rocks</a> is pressurized with carbon dioxide gas<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> at about 4,000 <a href="/wiki/Pascal_(unit)" title="Pascal (unit)">kPa</a> (40 <a href="/wiki/Bar_(unit)" title="Bar (unit)">bar</a>; 580 <a href="/wiki/Pound_per_square_inch" title="Pound per square inch">psi</a>). When placed in the mouth, it dissolves (just like other hard candy) and releases the gas bubbles with an audible pop.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Leavening_agent" title="Leavening agent">Leavening agents</a> cause dough to rise by producing carbon dioxide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Baker%27s_yeast" title="Baker's yeast">Baker's yeast</a> produces carbon dioxide by fermentation of sugars within the dough, while chemical leaveners such as <a href="/wiki/Baking_powder" title="Baking powder">baking powder</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baking_soda" title="Baking soda">baking soda</a> release carbon dioxide when heated or if exposed to <a href="/wiki/Acid" title="Acid">acids</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Beverages">Beverages</span></h4>
<p>Carbon dioxide is used to produce <a href="/wiki/Carbonation" title="Carbonation">carbonated</a> <a href="/wiki/Soft_drink" title="Soft drink">soft drinks</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soda_water" title="Soda water">soda water</a>. Traditionally, the carbonation of beer and sparkling wine came about through natural fermentation, but many manufacturers carbonate these drinks with carbon dioxide recovered from the fermentation process. In the case of bottled and kegged beer, the most common method used is carbonation with recycled carbon dioxide. With the exception of British <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cask_ale#Real_ale" title="Cask ale">real ale</a>, draught beer is usually transferred from kegs in a cold room or cellar to dispensing taps on the bar using pressurized carbon dioxide, sometimes mixed with nitrogen.
</p><p>The taste of soda water (and related taste sensations in other carbonated beverages) is an effect of the dissolved carbon dioxide rather than the bursting bubbles of the gas. <a href="/wiki/Carbonic_anhydrase_4" title="Carbonic anhydrase 4">Carbonic anhydrase 4</a> converts to <a href="/wiki/Carbonic_acid" title="Carbonic acid">carbonic acid</a> leading to a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sour" title="Sour">sour</a> taste, and also the dissolved carbon dioxide induces a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Somatosensory" title="Somatosensory">somatosensory</a> response.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Winemaking">Winemaking</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dry_ice_used_to_preserve_grapes_after_harvest.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2304" data-file-width="3072" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Dry_ice_used_to_preserve_grapes_after_harvest.jpg/220px-Dry_ice_used_to_preserve_grapes_after_harvest.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Dry_ice_used_to_preserve_grapes_after_harvest.jpg/330px-Dry_ice_used_to_preserve_grapes_after_harvest.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Dry_ice_used_to_preserve_grapes_after_harvest.jpg/440px-Dry_ice_used_to_preserve_grapes_after_harvest.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dry_ice_used_to_preserve_grapes_after_harvest.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Dry ice used to preserve grapes after harvest</div></div></div>
<p>Carbon dioxide in the form of <a href="/wiki/Dry_ice" title="Dry ice">dry ice</a> is often used during the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Cold_soak&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Cold soak (page does not exist)">cold soak</a> phase in <a href="/wiki/Winemaking" title="Winemaking">winemaking</a> to cool clusters of <a href="/wiki/Grape" title="Grape">grapes</a> quickly after picking to help prevent spontaneous <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fermentation_(wine)" title="Fermentation (wine)">fermentation</a> by wild <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yeast_(wine)" title="Yeast (wine)">yeast</a>. The main advantage of using dry ice over water ice is that it cools the grapes without adding any additional water that might decrease the sugar concentration in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grape_must" title="Grape must">grape must</a>, and thus the <a href="/wiki/Ethanol" title="Ethanol">alcohol</a> concentration in the finished wine. Carbon dioxide is also used to create a hypoxic environment for <a href="/wiki/Carbonic_maceration" title="Carbonic maceration">carbonic maceration</a>, the process used to produce <a href="/wiki/Beaujolais" title="Beaujolais">Beaujolais</a> wine.
</p><p>Carbon dioxide is sometimes used to top up wine bottles or other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Storage_(wine)" title="Storage (wine)">storage</a> vessels such as barrels to prevent oxidation, though it has the problem that it can dissolve into the wine, making a previously still wine slightly fizzy. For this reason, other gases such as <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a> or <a href="/wiki/Argon" title="Argon">argon</a> are preferred for this process by professional wine makers.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Stunning_animals">Stunning animals</span></h4>
<p>Carbon dioxide is often used to "stun" animals before slaughter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> "Stunning" may be a misnomer, as the animals are not knocked out immediately and may suffer distress.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Campbell_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Campbell-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Inert_gas">Inert gas</span></h3>
<p>Carbon dioxide is one of the most commonly used compressed gases for pneumatic (pressurized gas) systems in portable pressure tools. Carbon dioxide is also used as an atmosphere for <a href="/wiki/Welding" title="Welding">welding</a>, although in the welding arc, it reacts to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxidation" title="Oxidation">oxidize</a> most metals. Use in the automotive industry is common despite significant evidence that welds made in carbon dioxide are more <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brittle" title="Brittle">brittle</a> than those made in more inert atmospheres.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> When used for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MIG_welding" title="MIG welding">MIG welding</a>, CO<sub>2</sub> use is sometimes referred to as MAG welding, for Metal Active Gas, as CO<sub>2</sub> can react at these high temperatures. It tends to produce a hotter puddle than truly inert atmospheres, improving the flow characteristics. Although, this may be due to atmospheric reactions occurring at the puddle site. This is usually the opposite of the desired effect when welding, as it tends to embrittle the site, but may not be a problem for general mild steel welding, where ultimate ductility is not a major concern.
</p><p>Carbon dioxide is used in many consumer products that require pressurized gas because it is inexpensive and nonflammable, and because it undergoes a phase transition from gas to liquid at room temperature at an attainable pressure of approximately 60 <a href="/wiki/Bar_(unit)" title="Bar (unit)">bar</a> (870 <a href="/wiki/Pound_per_square_inch" title="Pound per square inch">psi</a>; 59 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atmosphere_(unit)" title="Atmosphere (unit)">atm</a>), allowing far more carbon dioxide to fit in a given container than otherwise would. Life jackets often contain canisters of pressured carbon dioxide for quick inflation. <a href="/wiki/Aluminium" title="Aluminium">Aluminium</a> capsules of CO<sub>2</sub> are also sold as supplies of compressed gas for <a href="/wiki/Air_gun" title="Air gun">air guns</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paintball" title="Paintball">paintball</a> markers/guns, inflating bicycle tires, and for making <a href="/wiki/Carbonated_water" title="Carbonated water">carbonated water</a>.  High concentrations of carbon dioxide can also be used to kill pests. Liquid carbon dioxide is used in <a href="/wiki/Supercritical_drying" title="Supercritical drying">supercritical drying</a> of some food products and technological materials, in the preparation of specimens for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scanning_electron_microscopy" title="Scanning electron microscopy">scanning electron microscopy</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nordestgaard_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nordestgaard-63">[63]</a></sup> and in the <a href="/wiki/Decaffeination" title="Decaffeination">decaffeination</a> of <a href="/wiki/Coffee_bean" title="Coffee bean">coffee beans</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fire_extinguisher">Fire extinguisher</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:US_Army_53023_Fire_Prevention_Week.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2848" data-file-width="4288" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/US_Army_53023_Fire_Prevention_Week.jpg/220px-US_Army_53023_Fire_Prevention_Week.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/US_Army_53023_Fire_Prevention_Week.jpg/330px-US_Army_53023_Fire_Prevention_Week.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/US_Army_53023_Fire_Prevention_Week.jpg/440px-US_Army_53023_Fire_Prevention_Week.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:US_Army_53023_Fire_Prevention_Week.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Use of a CO<sub>2</sub> fire extinguisher</div></div></div>
<p>Carbon dioxide can be used to extinguish flames by flooding the environment around the flame with the gas. It does not itself react to extinguish the flame, but starves the flame of oxygen by displacing it. Some <a href="/wiki/Fire_extinguisher#Halons,_Halon-replacement_clean_agents_and_carbon_dioxide" title="Fire extinguisher">fire extinguishers</a>, especially those designed for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_fire" title="Electrical fire">electrical fires</a>, contain liquid carbon dioxide under pressure. Carbon dioxide extinguishers work well on small flammable liquid and electrical fires, but not on ordinary combustible fires, because they do not cool the burning substances significantly, and when the carbon dioxide disperses, they can catch fire upon exposure to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atmospheric_oxygen" title="Atmospheric oxygen">atmospheric oxygen</a>. They are mainly used in server rooms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup>
</p><p>Carbon dioxide has also been widely used as an extinguishing agent in fixed fire-protection systems for local application of specific hazards and total flooding of a protected space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/International_Maritime_Organization" title="International Maritime Organization">International Maritime Organization</a> standards recognize carbon-dioxide systems for fire protection of ship holds and engine rooms. Carbon-dioxide-based fire-protection systems have been linked to several deaths, because it can cause suffocation in sufficiently high concentrations. A review of CO<sub>2</sub> systems identified 51 incidents between 1975 and the date of the report (2000), causing 72 deaths and 145 injuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Supercritical_CO2_as_solvent">Supercritical CO<sub>2</sub> as solvent</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Supercritical_carbon_dioxide" title="Supercritical carbon dioxide">Supercritical carbon dioxide</a> and <a href="/wiki/Green_chemistry" title="Green chemistry">Green chemistry</a></div>
<p>Liquid carbon dioxide is a good <a href="/wiki/Solvent" title="Solvent">solvent</a> for many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lipophilic" title="Lipophilic">lipophilic</a> <a href="/wiki/Organic_compound" title="Organic compound">organic compounds</a> and is used to remove <a href="/wiki/Caffeine" title="Caffeine">caffeine</a> from <a href="/wiki/Coffee" title="Coffee">coffee</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tsotsas_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-Tsotsas-17">[17]</a></sup> Carbon dioxide has attracted attention in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pharmaceutical" title="Pharmaceutical">pharmaceutical</a> and other chemical processing industries as a less toxic alternative to more traditional solvents such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organochloride" title="Organochloride">organochlorides</a>. It is also used by some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dry_cleaners" title="Dry cleaners">dry cleaners</a> for this reason. It is used in the preparation of some <a href="/wiki/Aerogel#Production" title="Aerogel">aerogels</a> because of the properties of supercritical carbon dioxide.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Medical_and_pharmacological_uses">Medical and pharmacological uses</span></h3>
<p>In medicine, up to 5% carbon dioxide (130 times atmospheric concentration) is added to oxygen for stimulation of breathing after <a href="/wiki/Apnea" title="Apnea">apnea</a> and to stabilize the <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>/CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> balance in blood.
</p><p>Carbon dioxide can be mixed with up to 50% oxygen, forming an inhalable gas; this is known as <a href="/wiki/Carbogen" title="Carbogen">Carbogen</a> and has a variety of medical and research uses.
</p><p>Another medical use are the <a href="/wiki/Mofetta" title="Mofetta">mofette</a>, dry spas that use carbon dioxide from post-volcanic discharge for therapeutic purposes.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Energy">Energy</span></h3>
<p>Supercritical CO<sub>2</sub> is used as the working fluid in the <a href="/wiki/Allam_power_cycle" title="Allam power cycle">Allam power cycle</a> engine.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Fossil_fuel_recovery">Fossil fuel recovery</span></h4>
<p>Carbon dioxide is used in <a href="/wiki/Enhanced_oil_recovery" title="Enhanced oil recovery">enhanced oil recovery</a> where it is injected into or adjacent to producing oil wells, usually under <a href="/wiki/Supercritical_fluid" title="Supercritical fluid">supercritical</a> conditions, when it becomes <a href="/wiki/Miscibility" title="Miscibility">miscible</a> with the oil. This approach can increase original oil recovery by reducing residual oil saturation by between 7% to 23% additional to <a href="/wiki/Extraction_of_petroleum#Primary_recovery" title="Extraction of petroleum">primary extraction</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> It acts as both a pressurizing agent and, when dissolved into the underground <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crude_oil" title="Crude oil">crude oil</a>, significantly reduces its viscosity, and changing surface chemistry enabling the oil to flow more rapidly through the reservoir to the removal well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> In mature oil fields, extensive pipe networks are used to carry the carbon dioxide to the injection points.
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Enhanced_coal_bed_methane_recovery" title="Enhanced coal bed methane recovery">enhanced coal bed methane recovery</a>, carbon dioxide would be pumped into the coal seam to displace methane, as opposed to current methods which primarily rely on the removal of water (to reduce pressure) to make the coal seam release its trapped methane.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Bio_transformation_into_fuel">Bio transformation into fuel</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Carbon_capture_and_utilization" title="Carbon capture and utilization">Carbon capture and utilization</a></div>
<p>It has been proposed that CO<sub>2</sub> from power generation be bubbled into ponds to stimulate growth of <a href="/wiki/Algae" title="Algae">algae</a> that could then be converted into <a href="/wiki/Biodiesel" title="Biodiesel">biodiesel</a> fuel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-csmon_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-csmon-70">[70]</a></sup> A strain of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cyanobacterium" title="Cyanobacterium">cyanobacterium</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Synechococcus_elongatus" title="Synechococcus elongatus">Synechococcus elongatus</a></i> has been genetically engineered to produce the fuels <a href="/wiki/Isobutyraldehyde" title="Isobutyraldehyde">isobutyraldehyde</a> and <a href="/wiki/Isobutanol" title="Isobutanol">isobutanol</a> from CO<sub>2</sub> using photosynthesis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p><p>Researchers have developed a process called electrolysis, using enzymes isolated from bacteria to power the chemical reactions which convert CO<sub>2</sub> into fuels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup>
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Refrigerant">Refrigerant</span></h5>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Refrigerant" title="Refrigerant">Refrigerant</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_automotive_air_conditioning" title="Sustainable automotive air conditioning">Sustainable automotive air conditioning</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:442px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Comparison_carbon_dioxide_water_phase_diagrams.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="224" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="193" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Comparison_carbon_dioxide_water_phase_diagrams.svg/440px-Comparison_carbon_dioxide_water_phase_diagrams.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Comparison_carbon_dioxide_water_phase_diagrams.svg/660px-Comparison_carbon_dioxide_water_phase_diagrams.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Comparison_carbon_dioxide_water_phase_diagrams.svg/880px-Comparison_carbon_dioxide_water_phase_diagrams.svg.png 2x" width="440"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Comparison_carbon_dioxide_water_phase_diagrams.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Comparison of the pressure–temperature phase diagrams of carbon dioxide (red) and water (blue) as a log-lin chart with phase transitions points at 1 atmosphere</div></div></div>
<p>Liquid and solid carbon dioxide are important <a href="/wiki/Refrigerant" title="Refrigerant">refrigerants</a>, especially in the food industry, where they are employed during the transportation and storage of ice cream and other frozen foods. Solid carbon dioxide is called "dry ice" and is used for small shipments where refrigeration equipment is not practical. Solid carbon dioxide is always below −78.5 °C (−109.3 °F) at regular atmospheric pressure, regardless of the air temperature.
</p><p><span class="anchor" id="R744"></span> Liquid carbon dioxide (industry nomenclature R744 or R-744) was used as a refrigerant prior to the use<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> of <a href="/wiki/Dichlorodifluoromethane" title="Dichlorodifluoromethane">dichlorodifluoromethane</a> (R12, a <a href="/wiki/Chlorofluorocarbon" title="Chlorofluorocarbon">chlorofluorocarbon</a> (CFC) compound). CO<sub style="font-size: 80%;vertical-align: -0.35em">2</sub> might enjoy a renaissance because one of the main substitutes to CFCs, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1,1,1,2-tetrafluoroethane" title="1,1,1,2-tetrafluoroethane">1,1,1,2-tetrafluoroethane</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/R134a" title="R134a">R134a</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Hydrofluorocarbon" title="Hydrofluorocarbon">hydrofluorocarbon</a> (HFC) compound) contributes to <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a> more than CO<sub style="font-size: 80%;vertical-align: -0.35em">2</sub> does. CO<sub style="font-size: 80%;vertical-align: -0.35em">2</sub> physical properties are highly favorable for cooling, refrigeration, and heating purposes, having a high volumetric cooling capacity. Due to the need to operate at pressures of up to 130 bars (1,900 psi; 13,000 kPa), CO<sub style="font-size: 80%;vertical-align: -0.35em">2</sub> systems require highly mechanically resistant reservoirs and components that have already been developed for mass production in many sectors. In automobile air conditioning, in more than 90% of all driving conditions for latitudes higher than 50°, CO<sub style="font-size: 80%;vertical-align: -0.35em">2</sub> (R744) operates more efficiently than systems using HFCs (e.g., R134a). Its environmental advantages (<a href="/wiki/Global_warming_potential" title="Global warming potential">GWP</a> of 1, non-ozone depleting, non-toxic, non-flammable) could make it the future working fluid to replace current HFCs in cars, supermarkets, and heat pump water heaters, among others. <a href="/wiki/Coca-Cola" title="Coca-Cola">Coca-Cola</a> has fielded CO<sub style="font-size: 80%;vertical-align: -0.35em">2</sub>-based beverage coolers and the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Army" title="United States Army">U.S. Army</a> is interested in CO<sub style="font-size: 80%;vertical-align: -0.35em">2</sub> refrigeration and heating technology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ccref1_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-ccref1-75">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usforces_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-usforces-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Minor_uses">Minor uses</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Carbon_Dioxide_Laser_At_The_Laser_Effects_Test_Facility.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="960" decoding="async" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Carbon_Dioxide_Laser_At_The_Laser_Effects_Test_Facility.jpg/300px-Carbon_Dioxide_Laser_At_The_Laser_Effects_Test_Facility.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Carbon_Dioxide_Laser_At_The_Laser_Effects_Test_Facility.jpg/450px-Carbon_Dioxide_Laser_At_The_Laser_Effects_Test_Facility.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Carbon_Dioxide_Laser_At_The_Laser_Effects_Test_Facility.jpg/600px-Carbon_Dioxide_Laser_At_The_Laser_Effects_Test_Facility.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Carbon_Dioxide_Laser_At_The_Laser_Effects_Test_Facility.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Carbon-dioxide_laser" title="Carbon-dioxide laser">carbon-dioxide laser</a></div></div></div>
<p>Carbon dioxide is the <a href="/wiki/Active_laser_medium" title="Active laser medium">lasing medium</a> in a <a href="/wiki/Carbon-dioxide_laser" title="Carbon-dioxide laser">carbon-dioxide laser</a>, which is one of the earliest type of lasers.
</p><p>Carbon dioxide can be used as a means of controlling the <a href="/wiki/PH" title="PH">pH</a> of swimming pools,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> by continuously adding gas to the water, thus keeping the pH from rising. Among the advantages of this is the avoidance of handling (more hazardous) acids. Similarly, it is also used in the maintaining <a href="/wiki/Reef_aquarium" title="Reef aquarium">reef aquaria</a>, where it is commonly used in <a href="/wiki/Calcium_reactor" title="Calcium reactor">calcium reactors</a> to temporarily lower the pH of water being passed over <a href="/wiki/Calcium_carbonate" title="Calcium carbonate">calcium carbonate</a> in order to allow the calcium carbonate to dissolve into the water more freely, where it is used by some <a href="/wiki/Coral" title="Coral">corals</a> to build their skeleton.
</p><p>Used as the primary coolant in the British <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Advanced_gas-cooled_reactor" title="Advanced gas-cooled reactor">advanced gas-cooled reactor</a> for nuclear power generation.
</p><p>Carbon dioxide induction is commonly used for the euthanasia of laboratory research animals. Methods to administer CO<sub>2</sub> include placing animals directly into a closed, prefilled chamber containing CO<sub>2</sub>, or exposure to a gradually increasing concentration of CO<sub>2</sub>. The <a href="/wiki/American_Veterinary_Medical_Association" title="American Veterinary Medical Association">American Veterinary Medical Association</a>'s 2020 guidelines for carbon dioxide induction state that a displacement rate of 30% to 70% of the chamber or cage volume per minute is optimal for the humane euthanasia of small rodents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-avma_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-avma-78">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 5, 31">: 5, 31 </span></sup> Percentages of CO<sub>2</sub> vary for different species, based on identified optimal percentages to minimize distress.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-avma_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-avma-78">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 22">: 22 </span></sup>
</p><p>Carbon dioxide is also used in several related <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_cleaning" title="Carbon dioxide cleaning">cleaning and surface-preparation</a> techniques.
</p>
<h2><span id="In_Earth.27s_atmosphere"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="In_Earth's_atmosphere">In Earth's atmosphere</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_in_Earth%27s_atmosphere" title="Carbon dioxide in Earth's atmosphere">Carbon dioxide in Earth's atmosphere</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carbon_cycle" title="Carbon cycle">Carbon cycle</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mauna_Loa_CO2_monthly_mean_concentration.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="708" data-file-width="708" decoding="async" height="300" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Mauna_Loa_CO2_monthly_mean_concentration.svg/300px-Mauna_Loa_CO2_monthly_mean_concentration.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Mauna_Loa_CO2_monthly_mean_concentration.svg/450px-Mauna_Loa_CO2_monthly_mean_concentration.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Mauna_Loa_CO2_monthly_mean_concentration.svg/600px-Mauna_Loa_CO2_monthly_mean_concentration.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mauna_Loa_CO2_monthly_mean_concentration.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Keeling_curve" title="Keeling curve">Keeling curve</a> of the atmospheric CO<sub>2</sub> concentration<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Carbon dioxide in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth%27s_atmosphere" title="Earth's atmosphere">Earth's atmosphere</a> is a <a href="/wiki/Trace_gas" title="Trace gas">trace gas</a>, having a global average concentration of 415 parts per million by volume (or 630 parts per million by mass) as of the end of year 2020.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NOAA_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-NOAA-80">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-theguardian_mar_2016_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-theguardian_mar_2016-81">[81]</a></sup> Atmospheric CO<sub style="font-size: 80%;vertical-align: -0.35em">2</sub> concentrations fluctuate slightly with the seasons, falling during the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Hemisphere" title="Northern Hemisphere">Northern Hemisphere</a> spring and summer as plants consume the gas and rising during northern autumn and winter as plants go dormant or die and decay. Concentrations also vary on a regional basis, most strongly <a href="/wiki/Planetary_boundary_layer" title="Planetary boundary layer">near the ground</a> with much smaller variations aloft.
